          1.      INTRODUCTION
          RWDI was retained by Hines to conduct a noise study for its proposed developments at 45/141 Bay
          Street in Toronto, Ontario. The purpose of this assessment was to evaluate sound levels at the site due
          to adjacent road traffic, rail traffic and stationary sources, as well as acoustic impact of the development.
          Results were compared to the MOE “Publication NPC-300, Stationary and Transportation Sources –
          Approval and Planning” guidelines. An indication of general sound level reductions needed to meet the
          guideline criteria for the worst-case façade locations of the 45 Bay Street Tower.


          2.      DESCRIPTION OF PROJECT AND SITE
          In partnership with Metrolinx, Ivanhoé Cambridge is proposing to construct a mixed-use office
          development at 45 Bay Street that features a bus transit terminal and ancillary retail uses. The proposed
          mixed-use development includes a 48-storey office tower and a seven-storey podium facing onto 45 Bay
          Street. A Metrolinx bus terminal is integrated into the eastern portion of the tower podium, with direct
          pedestrian connectivity to Union Station to the east. The bus terminal has access and frontage on Lake
          Shore Boulevard. The podium also features ancillary retail and amenity spaces on level four. An elevated
          landscaped open space bridges the rail corridor to the north, which features a small entry and vertical
          circulation at 141 Bay Street, immediately north of the rail corridor.

          The tower and podium include a total of approximately 135,000 square metres of non-residential gross
          floor area, including approximately 10,000 square metres for bus terminal uses and approximately 7,800
          square metres for ancillary retail and amenity uses. The elevated park is proposed as a publicly
          accessible open space, with landscaping, seating and unimpeded views of the city skyline to the east and
          west. The development features two grade-level plazas along Bay Street, one at 141 Bay in front of the
          bridge entry and one in front of 45 Bay that wraps around the Lake Shore Boulevard frontage. The plazas
          are connected by an enhanced Bay East Teamway at grade, which continues to be a publicly-accessible
          portion of the PATH network. A pedestrian bridge will also extend the PATH network across Bay Street
          south of the rail corridor, providing a continuous PATH connection on level two from the Air Canada
          Centre east toward Yonge Street.

          The 45 Bay Street structure is the first phase of a larger mixed-use office development. A second phase
          of development being considered would include a second office tower at 141 Bay Street, immediately
          north of the rail corridor.


          3.      APPLICABLE GUIDELINES
          The detailed evaluation of road traffic, rail traffic and stationary source related sound levels were
          assessed using MOE Publication NPC-300, “Stationary and Transportation Sources – Approval and
          Planning” dated October 2013 (NPC-300).




Reputation Resources Results               Canada | USA | UK | India | China | Hong Kong | Singapore             www.rwdi.com
                              45/141 Bay Street Development
                              Environmental Noise Study
                              RWDI #1401125
                              September 25, 2014                                                                      Page 2




          3.1         MOE Publication NPC-300
          3.1.1       Road and Rail Traffic

          The assessment of road and rail traffic-related sound under MOE Publication NPC-300 does not usually
          consider retail and office spaces, as these are not normally considered noise sensitive. However,
          Publication NPC-300 provides supplementary sound level criteria for information and good design
          practice, as provided in Table 1. Daytime sound level limits for commercial uses are related to the type of
          usage: i.e. open-plan offices, semi-private offices and conference rooms. These limits are applied
          indoors.

                     The daytime sound level limits for road-traffic sources for open plan offices areas is an LEQ,16hr of
                      50 dBA, and the limit applied to semi-private offices and conference rooms is LEQ,16hr of 45 dBA.

                     The daytime sound level limits for rail-traffic sources for open plan offices areas is an L EQ,16hr of
                      45 dBA, and the limit applied to semi-private offices and conference rooms is LEQ,16hr of 40 dBA.

          The NPC-300 sound level criteria for road and rail-traffic sources are summarized in Table 1.

          3.1.2       Stationary Sources

          Off-site sources of stationary sound such as nearby Snow Clearing Devices (SCD) are assessed
          separately using guidance from NPC-300. Since no specific limits are provided for stationary-source
          impact on in office or retail space, the limits for sensitive spaces are used as information for good design
          objectives. For stationary sources, the limits are provided for the façade of the building rather than
          indoors. The exclusionary limits in a major urban center such as this are 50 dBA for day-time (0700–
          2300h) and 45 dBA for night-time (2300-0700). Based on MOE guidance, indoor sound level limits which
          are 10 dB below outdoor façade limits are applied, as shown in Table 1.

          Table 1: NPC-300 Indoor Sound Level Criteria for Non-Sensitive Land Uses.
                                                                                   LEQ (Time Period) (dBA)
                       Type of Space                Time Period        Road             Rail        Stationary Sources
                                                                     (LEQ, 16hr)      (LEQ, 16hr)         (LEQ, 1hr)
              General offices, reception areas,                                                                   1
                                                    07:00-23:00         50               45                  40
              retail stores, etc.
              Individual or semi-private offices,                                                                 1
                                                    07:00-23:00         45               40                  40
              conference rooms, reading rooms

          1
           Indoor limits taken to be 10 dB below applicable NPC-300 limit for day-time/evening operation at the building façade
          of 50dBA.

          4.      NOISE IMPACT ASSESSMENT
          Numerical analysis has been conducted that estimates the sound levels from surrounding sources on the
          proposed developments. The impact of the development on its surroundings and on itself is addressed
          non-numerically.

          4.1     Noise Receptors

          The development consists entirely of office and retail spaces, which under MOE Publication NPC-300 are
          not described as noise sensitive spaces. Numerical modeling has been conducted to determine sound
          levels at worst-case locations on the various faces of the tower façade. Comparison of the outdoor levels
          with the indoor criteria has been used to establish the overall noise reduction needs. Once further details
          of the buildings have been developed, it may be desirable to refine the analysis.

          One worst case receptor corresponding to each building face was selected based on the highest impact
          of road-traffic noise, which is the strongest contributor to sound levels on the façade. The locations of the
          four modelled receptors are shown in Figure 1. These receptors were also used to assess sound from
          rail-traffic and stationary sources. The heights of receptors have been assessed at 50% of the maximum
          occupied height of tower, in accordance with NPC-300. Lower receptor heights on the 45 Bay Street
          building will have some screening due to the edges of the podium, and will therefore have slightly lower
          sound levels.

          4.2     Source Data
          4.2.1   Road Traffic Data

          The five roadways that have potential to influence the proposed development site are Yonge Street, Front
          Street, Bay Street, Lake Shore Boulevard and the Gardiner Expressway. Traffic data was provided by
          the City of Toronto, dated May 7, 2014, is summarized in Table 2 and attached in Appendix A. As the
          data did not include a classification breakdown for the Gardiner Expressway, a breakdown of 80/5/15 was
          used for light/medium/heavy vehicles, respectively. A 2% annual rate of road-traffic growth was assumed
          on all roadways over a 10-year projection period.

          4.2.2   Rail Traffic Data

          Rail traffic data for the Union Station Rail Corridor (USRC) was supplied by GO Transit/Metrolinx on
          August 28, 2014. The data is provided in Appendix B and summarized in Table 2. Future 2024 quanties
          for GO Transit trains was provided by GO Transit/Metrolinx. Other rail traffic was projected to a 10-year
          horizon using an annual rail-traffic growth rate of 2%. Commuter rail traffic was assumed to be equally
          distributed over 14 rail lines with passenger platform access, and freight traffic was assumed to use the
          rail lines at the south extreme of the USRC. The speed limit on the Union Station Corridor is currently 16
          km/h (10 mph), but future planned switching and signaling upgrades will allow for a future speed limit of
          73 km/h (45 mph). At the request of GO Transit/ Metrolinx, a 73 km/h train speed has been used as a
          predictable worst case scenario for all rail traffic to assess noise impacts.

          Noise sources associated with idling train traffic at Union Station, as well as queuing of trains to the east
          of the station, were assessed as sources of rail noise. Idling train locations are shown in Figure 2. Idling
          was assessed using a total of 14 idling commuter locomotives located at the east end of Union Station
          passenger platform during the day-time period, and 7 locomotives during the night-time period. Each
          idling locomotive was assumed to operate for 15 minutes out of each hour.

          4.2.3         Off-Site Stationary Source Data

          Stationary noise sources considered for the assessment are rail switch/track heaters in operation at
          various critical rail switches to the east and west of Union station. Switch heater locations are shown in
          Figure 2. Manufacturer’s data of equivalent equipment, provided in Appendix C, was used to determine
          sound power level of a representative unit. A total of 144 track heater units were considering in the vicinity
          of the development, with 76 units to the east, and 68 units to the west.

          4.3           Noise Modelling
          4.3.1         Road-Traffic Modelling

          Sound levels from road traffic were calculated using Ontario Road Noise Analysis Method for
          Environment and Transportation (ORNAMENT) (MOE, 1989). The locations of the four modelled
          receptors are shown in Figure 1. Sample ORNAMENT sound level calculations are provided in Appendix
          D. Table 3 summarizes the predicted road-traffic sound levels at the various building faces.

          Table 3: Results of ORNAMENT Road Traffic NOISE Modelling.

                     45 Bay




                                 East Façade              78                     73           78                 73
                                 South Façade             81                     76           81                 76
                                 West Façade              76                     71           76                 71

          The greatest predicted impact of road-traffic noise on the 45 Bay Street Tower is on the south façade,
          with outdoor sound levels at the façade of 81 dBA for the day-time and 76 dBA night-time. This is due
          primarily to the high degree of exposure to the Gardiner Expressway and Lake Shore Boulevard to the
          south. Receptors on the east and west building faces also have substantial exposure to these higher
          traffic roadways, whereas the receptor associated with the north building face is shielded, and has
          contributions of road-traffic noise primarily from the lower traffic roadways of Yonge Street, Front Street
          and Bay Street. Shielding effects from the construction of the 141 Bay Street Tower to the north (Phase 2)
          has no significant effect on sound levels at the various façades of the 45 Bay Street Tower.

          4.3.2         Rail-Traffic Modelling

          Sound levels associated with rail-traffic sources were predicted using both Sound from Trains
          Environmental Analysis Method (STEAM) (MOE, 1990) and Cadna/A. STEAM was used for moving
          trains while Cadna/A was used for the trains that were stopped and idling. Rail-traffic sound is the sum of
          the two.

          4.4      Addressing Excess Sound

          Suggested good design practice criteria provided in NPC-300 were used to determine desirable sound
          level reductions for the building façades. Under NPC-300, rail, road & stationary noise sources are
          assessed separately. Thus the façade requirement for each is calculated individually. However, the
          reduction for each of the source-types is then combined to obtain the Overall Façade Noise Reduction for
          each façade. These noise reductions are outlined in Tables 8 and 9, for semi-private offices/conference
          rooms and open-plan offices, respectively.

          Table 8: Overall Façade Noise Reductions of Building Façade Due to Road, Rail and Stationary Sources
          for Semi-Private Offices or Conference Rooms.

                                           West Façade              33                    28         33                28

          Overall façade noise reductions vary between 33 dB and 36 dB on the various façade faces adjacent to
          semi-private offices and conference rooms, and vary between 28 dB and 31 dB on the façade faces
          adjacent to open-plan offices. These represent substantial façade constructions if the indoor sound level
          criteria are to be met.

          4.5     Impact of the Development
          4.5.1   Impact on Surroundings

          The impact of noise from the development on its surroundings is expected to be small. Traffic noise
          related to the proposed development will be small in relation to the traffic noise on the major roads
          immediately adjacent to the development. Traffic noise is therefore not of concern. Noise from
          mechanical equipment associated with the development is expected to be small due to the office nature
          of the proposed development.

          4.5.2   Impact on Itself

          Consideration should be given to control air-borne and structure-born noise generated within the
          proposed development. Within the development itself, the main sources of noise that are likely to affect
          the uses of the building, are the mechanical systems. The following are the general areas where attention
          may be needed at the final design stages. As the design progresses, an acoustical consultant should be
          retained to review the plans and provide design suggestions.

          Building rooms or spaces next to mechanical equipment areas may be adversely affected by sound
          transmitted through ducts, opening, or noise induced by the vibrations of adjoining walls. The isolation of
          sound from mechanical equipment can be readily achieved by good design.

          All supply, return and miscellaneous fans should be provided with adequate vibration isolation to ensure
          that vibration is not transferred to the building structure and become a source of noise. Duct silencers
          can be used to ensure that high fan noise levels are not carried by the duct work to residential and other
          noise sensitive rooms throughout the building. Fans should be connected to ducting with flexible
          connectors. Duct work should be hung on vibration isolating hangers.




Reputation Resources Results                     Canada | USA | UK | India | China | Hong Kong | Singapore                 www.rwdi.com
                           45/141 Bay Street Development
                           Environmental Noise Study
                           RWDI #1401125
                           September 25, 2014                                                                 Page 9




          All chillers, compressors and similar items of equipment should be provided with adequate vibration
          isolation and mounted on concrete inertia bases. If these units are to be located above grade on the roof,
          the structure must be designed to hold the weight of the unit and inertia block. Mechanical rooms or
          penthouses containing these units may need a floating floor or other alternate acoustically equivalent
          “room to room” construction to ensure that the high sound levels associated with these items are not
          transmitted to offices and other spaces.

          All piping runs within the building are potential sources of noise. For example, plumbing can be a source
          of noise particularly if the source is not in the same suite as the listener. Pipes that pass through walls,
          floors and ceiling should be treated to reduce potential noise and vibration impacts. For example, pipes
          should be hung on vibration isolating hangers, and risers should not be rigidly connected to the floors or
          other supporting members at anchor locations.

          Pumps should be provided with adequate vibration isolation and mounted on concrete inertia bases
          where required. Transformers and other vibration noise producing electrical components should be
          provided with adequate vibration isolation.

          Walls and floors separating mechanical rooms, fan rooms, electrical rooms, elevators shafts and rooms,
          garbage chutes, retail spaces etc. from occupied spaces in the building should have adequate sound
          transmission loss. Attention should be paid to noise from rooftop mechanical spaces penetrating the roof
          slab and entering spaces below. Similarly, noise from vehicle activities directly below occupied spaces
          should be considered as part of detailed design.


          5.      CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
          This assessment of sound from the surrounding environment on the proposed development has been
          considered in detail. The proposed developments are affected predominantly by road traffic along the
          Gardiner Expressway and Lake Shore Boulevard, as well as commuter and freight rail traffic along the
          USRC. Good design practice recommendations are provided for each of the building façades.
          Substantial façade constructions will be needed to meet the indoor sound level criteria.

          The impact of the proposed development on the surroundings has not been assessed numerically. Due
          to the high levels of sound from road and rail traffic in the area, the sound from stationary sources at the
          development is expected to be comparatively insignificant. Vehicles at the proposed development are not
          expected to have impacts notably greater than those already present. Since further assessment of the
          facility on its surroundings is not expected to produce results of significance, detailed analysis of on-site
          sources is not considered necessary for technical purposes.

Revised Railway Maximum Speed


From: Adam Snow

<Michael.Johnston@gotransit.com>
Subject: RE: Vibration Measurements - Union Station - RWDI


Hello David

Just to close the ‘data request’ component of this process, I wanted to provide an update on train speeds. A 10 mph
limit was previously noted for trains in the vicinity of the USRC train shed. While this is close to the actual planned
limit of 15 mph in the immediate area, there are plans for speeds to reach as high as 45 mph on some tracks in the
area under the planned signal improvement program through the corridor, and we would want to protect for such an
option. Consequently to be conservative the speed considered for your analysis should be 45 mph.

Please let me know if you have any questions.

Thanks,

Adam

Train Traffic Volume


From: Adam Snow [mailto:Adam.Snow@gotransit.com]
Sent: August-28-14 3:48 PM
To: David Arthurs
Cc: Kyle Hellewell; Jordan Gilmour; Justin Diamond; Bill Moffatt; Michael Johnston; Jeff Bateman
Subject: RE: Vibration Measurements - Union Station - RWDI

Hello David – Further to our recent discussions regarding your information request which was first sent to me on July
30, I see that Michael has provided answers to some of your questions at the bottom of this email.

With respect to rail traffic data, GO Transit Planning has developed comprehensive forecasts for each of the GO lines
on a midterm (extending to approximately 2024) basis. As a result you do not have to apply a growth factor to existing
train traffic as you had suggested.

The preliminary weekday commuter train volume forecast for the east side of the Union Station train shed combines
train traffic from three different GO lines - Richmond Hill, Stouffville and Lakeshore East. The total traffic count would
be in the order of 304 trains (247 day, 57 night). Trains will be comprised of a single locomotive and up to 12
passenger cars.

I note that these forecasts are subject to change and depend on service planning decisions, funding availability and
passenger demand.

It should be noted that some GO trains may occasionally idle on the tracks to the east of the train shed, particularly
during peak periods, and the noise study should address the noise implications of this activity. In light of the fact that
idling levels will vary depending on prevailing conditions, it is not possible to identify specific frequencies and
durations. Field observations would assist with developing an understanding of such activity, although it should also
be noted that patterns may change in the future with planned service and infrastructure improvements.

Some critical switches in the vicinity of the subject site are kept clear of snow using blowers mounted at track level.
GO Transit also owns a snow blower that is used to clear snow within the Union Station Rail Corridor. There are
noise implications associated with this equipment, although again that frequency and duration of same would depend
on prevailing weather conditions.

With respect to your proposed vibration measurements, we can coordinate access to facilitate your measurements
when you are ready. Perhaps a conference call between you and I and Justin Diamond would be useful to set out the
parameters for this work. Please provide a few times that would work for you next week.

Please feel free to contact me with any additional questions.

Thanks,

Adam


Adam Snow
Third Party Projects Officer

          APPENDIX C
Stationary Source – Snow Clearing Devices (SCDs)


From: Adam Snow [mailto:Adam.Snow@gotransit.com]
Sent: September-09-14 12:47 PM
To: David Arthurs
Cc: Justin Diamond; Peter VanDelden; Kyle Hellewell; Katherine Latimer; Jeff Bateman; Michael Johnston
Subject: RE: Vibration Measurements - Union Station - RWDI

Hello David – Further to our on-going exchange of information regarding acoustical matters in the vicinity of the
proposed overbuild project at 45/141 Bay, I have assembled some materials regarding the fixed Switch Clearing
Devices (SCDs) that are installed in the study area. Based on my research, the information we have is relatively
sparse but here’s what we know:

*The attached diagram ‘UC50-C-SG10006-R6.pdf’ illustrates the location of the SCDs in the Union Station Rail
Corridor (USRC). The drawing is conceptual so some caution is required in assessing locations, but in general if you
zoom in to the area west of Yonge (where the development will be located), you will see that there are only a limited
number of SCDs in this location. Furthermore, I note that those on the north side of the corridor, in close proximity to
the train shed, will become redundant with the overbuild in place as the tracks will no longer be exposed to the
elements.
*Cross-referencing the plan with the attached spreadsheet ‘USRC SCD Update Rev1.xlsx’ (‘Scott’ tab) you will see
that most of the SCDs in the area are of the Hovey HF400 variety. I understand that we do not have noise level
information for the HF400 model, but we do have some information on the HF900 model which is louder than the
HF400 version. Sound level information for the HF900 is summarized in the attached ‘Hovey HF900 Sound Pressure
Test.pdf’ figure. This could be regarded as a ‘worst-case’ scenario for SCD noise.
*As noted previously these SCDs activate as dictated by prevailing weather conditions and as such the frequency
and duration of use is unpredictable. Given these circumstances such equipment is typically excluded from MOE
analyses. That said we recommend that you consider this equipment within the context of your analysis to be
conservative.
*As noted previously, the mobile snow blower is no longer used in the USRC and therefore does not need to be
considered in your study.

I trust that this information is useful.

Further to my email of last Friday please let us know when you would like to discuss your requirements and/or
conduct a site visit. We can arrange participation by representatives from GO and AECOM.

Thanks,

Adam
